<div id="review_1449168369" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1449168369" />
    <a title="Nicky" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/223837-nicky"><img alt="Nicky" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1308224598p2/223837.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1449168369?book_show_action=true">Nov 22, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nicky" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nicky" href="/user/show/223837-nicky">Nicky</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780553116007" href="/book/show/1942596.The_Tombs_of_Atuan">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/223837-nicky?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1449168369" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2326180661868237957">This always used to be my favourite of the series, both for sheer atmosphere and because it featured a female-centred world, in complete contrast to the first book. It’s almost the opposite, in that way: Ged isn’t the POV character anymore, and instead we follow Arha/Tenar, seeing her experience in a different land, seeing Ged as an outsider. That latter is especially fun, because though he talks about not learning Ogion’s lessons, it seems that he really has. And there was always an attraction </span>
  <span id="freeText2326180661868237957" style="display:none">This always used to be my favourite of the series, both for sheer atmosphere and because it featured a female-centred world, in complete contrast to the first book. It’s almost the opposite, in that way: Ged isn’t the POV character anymore, and instead we follow Arha/Tenar, seeing her experience in a different land, seeing Ged as an outsider. That latter is especially fun, because though he talks about not learning Ogion’s lessons, it seems that he really has. And there was always an attraction for the dark rituals, Arha’s dance in front of the Empty Throne, the drums struck softly at heart-pace. Le Guin didn’t just blindly throw together a bunch of superstitions and fake rituals: it hangs together as a cohesive whole, and the fact that even the characters find the rituals meaningless, strange, the significances lost in time… that also works for me.<br /><br />One image that always sticks with me is that of Ged asleep on the ground, the small thistle by his hand. That image somehow epitomises the book for me: his serenity and trust, his link to the world around him, and also the way Tenar sees him, truly sees him, alive and in the world and not at all a part of the dark existence she led before… it’s hard to put into words, but that image does it.<br /><br /><a href="http://breathesbooks.com/2015/12/06/review-the-tombs-of-atuan/" rel="nofollow noopener">Originally posted here.</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="2326180661868237957" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_63268353" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/63268353" />
    <a title="Nicky" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/223837-nicky"><img alt="Nicky" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1308224598p2/223837.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/63268353?book_show_action=true">Jul 13, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nicky" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nicky" href="/user/show/223837-nicky">Nicky</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/223837-nicky?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/223837-nicky?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer63268353" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13471856832107130933">Much as I love A Wizard of Earthsea, there isn't much feminine about it. It's a male society, it seems in that book, shaped by men and only inhabited by women. I don't know how much thought Le Guin put into that, originally, but the women in the story don't really have much of a place. There's the witch and Serret and the Kargish woman and Yarrow... but they don't have great parts in Ged's life. He's taken away from the tutelage of the witch because only a man can teach him wizardry, and there's</span>
  <span id="freeText13471856832107130933" style="display:none">Much as I love A Wizard of Earthsea, there isn't much feminine about it. It's a male society, it seems in that book, shaped by men and only inhabited by women. I don't know how much thought Le Guin put into that, originally, but the women in the story don't really have much of a place. There's the witch and Serret and the Kargish woman and Yarrow... but they don't have great parts in Ged's life. He's taken away from the tutelage of the witch because only a man can teach him wizardry, and there's the sayings, "Weak as women's magic" and "Wicked as women's magic". Le Guin addresses those issues later, in Tehanu, but women aren't really present in the first book.<br /><br />So it's just nice to have a book framed by women: Tenar and Thar and Kossil and Penthe, the priestesses and novices of the Tombs. Women are the only ones allowed to serve the gods, or at least the Nameless Ones -- well, women and eunuchs. The fact that Arha/Tenar is the main character, and not Ged, gives it a whole different slant. She has a different kind of life, so her story is rather different. Her story is less of an epic quest than Ged's -- there's tension, and danger, but they're not going to something, they're escaping something. She has to grow as a person in a different way. The quest is Ged's, as before, but we see him coming in from outside this time. It's interesting.<br /><br />The language and descriptions and images are all as beautiful as the first book. There's something very compelling about the Tombs, the dark rituals. You can feel the cold, the routine hardness -- you feel stuck in the rut that Arha has been stuck in throughout her many lives. You can feel the slow unchangingness of the place. And you feel the joy and weight of the escape, too. I like the rhythms and tastes of this book the best in the whole series, I think. Some of the descriptions have just stuck in my head -- the drum struck at a slow heart-pace, the little thistle growing beside Ged's hand. And some of the things Ged says, his descriptions of Havnor and his speech that is essentially about "nature red in tooth and claw".<br /><br />This is really the only book that steps out of Ged's own culture. The others are mostly rooted in the Archipelagan traditions, which is interesting enough, but this provides a bit more worldbuilding. Which is awesome.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13471856832107130933" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer2808195640802592430">I've read the first three Earthsea books a heap of times, starting when I was at my academic peak (i.e. in primary school). Through-out my childhood readings I preferred the two that sandwiched this one. Looking back it is easy for me to see why: it wasn't about Ged and it didn't have enough sailing about to far flung places (i.e. exploration) in it. In contrast, I have observed that a number of female Goodreaders who are also LeGuin fans, rate this higher than the other two. I can take a guess </span>
  <span id="freeText2808195640802592430" style="display:none">I've read the first three Earthsea books a heap of times, starting when I was at my academic peak (i.e. in primary school). Through-out my childhood readings I preferred the two that sandwiched this one. Looking back it is easy for me to see why: it wasn't about Ged and it didn't have enough sailing about to far flung places (i.e. exploration) in it. In contrast, I have observed that a number of female Goodreaders who are also LeGuin fans, rate this higher than the other two. I can take a guess as to why that might be; there are no female characters in the other two. There are some women, even some who play pivotal roles, in both, but they are not fully formed characters, let alone protagonists. Most of these women are unpleasant or down-right evil. A Wizard of Earthsea and The Farthest Shore are entirely male-dominated. <div id="review_1572174178" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1572174178" />
    <a title="Zanna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22591633-zanna"><img alt="Zanna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1512813535p2/22591633.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1572174178?book_show_action=true">May 01, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Zanna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Zanna" href="/user/show/22591633-zanna">Zanna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22591633-zanna?shelf=bechdel-pass">bechdel-pass</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1572174178" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14731330032350059856">To me the most beautiful and striking aspect of this haunting and haunted novel is the hesitantly built and fragile trust between Ged and Tenar. Without this trust, without each other’s help, neither of them could get anywhere, could even survive. In her retrospective afterword, Le Guin writes that at the time she wrote the novel she could not imagine a woman being truly independent, and her resolution emphasises interdependence between men and women. She makes the gendered interpretation of thi</span>
  <span id="freeText14731330032350059856" style="display:none">To me the most beautiful and striking aspect of this haunting and haunted novel is the hesitantly built and fragile trust between Ged and Tenar. Without this trust, without each other’s help, neither of them could get anywhere, could even survive. In her retrospective afterword, Le Guin writes that at the time she wrote the novel she could not imagine a woman being truly independent, and her resolution emphasises interdependence between men and women. She makes the gendered interpretation of this interdependence explicit, but although gender is very salient in the novel, I did not feel, for example, that Ged need necessarily have been male for the story to work. Of course, I am living in a different socio-political era. In any case, I was moved by their relationship, intense but not at all sexual, and the moment when Ged hands over his name is a worthy payoff for the many dreary chapters my co-reader and I had ploughed through to get to it.<br /><br />What also struck me reading the afterword though, was Le Guin’s insistence that the fantasy world of Earthsea had to reflect real-world patriarchy to have any political resonance. This contrasts with her treatment of skin colour in Earthsea and in other areas of her fantasy world, where protagonists are often dark-skinned but white supremacy seems simply not to be a thing. To me this, shall we say, <i>fantasy colourblindness</i> seems a nice strategy compared to the disturbingly all-white or worse universes produced by some fantasy authors. Le Guin reproduces patriarchy in order to critique it, but although suspicion of strangers or foreigners exists in Earthsea, it has nothing to do with melanin. Fantasy colourblindness functions like colourblind casting, suggesting that while racism is a major structuring element of the world we know, it is absurd and artificial. White readers like myself can learn something from our own surprise when a character we've already identified with is described as dark-skinned. <br /><br />Tenar, the supposedly reincarnated First Priestess of the Tombs bears the name Arha, which signifies that the Nameless Ones, creatures related to the shadow Ged invokes and is pursued by in A Wizard of Earthsea, have eaten her soul. Ged reassures her that this isn’t possible; that she is not evil, not corrupted or corruptible in the way she fears. The soul, he implies, cannot be eaten. This reminded me of Ishiguro’s <i>Never Let Me Go</i>, where the clones are made to produce art in the hopes that this will demonstrate that they have souls. What was so clear in that book, so incontrovertibly evident, was that the soul as it’s felt in my culture at least, is not something magical that could somehow be missing in someone conceived by artificial methods, but an emergent and dynamic phenomenon, relational, cumulative, arising from will, memory, emotion, contact with others, something no demon-invoking ritual could cancel out, something indestructible.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14731330032350059856" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2293049537" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2293049537" />
    <a title="RJ from the LBC" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/131730-rj-from-the-lbc"><img alt="RJ from the LBC" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605827139p2/131730.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2293049537?book_show_action=true">Aug 18, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="RJ from the LBC" class="user" itemprop="url" name="RJ from the LBC" href="/user/show/131730-rj-from-the-lbc">RJ from the LBC</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/36521839-the-tombs-of-atuan">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/131730-rj-from-the-lbc?shelf=guardian-1000-read">guardian-1000-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2293049537" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10151891592848502744"><b>What she had begun to learn was the weight of liberty. Freedom is a heavy load, a great and strange burden for the spirit to undertake. It is not easy. It is not a gift given, but a choice made, and the choice may be a hard one. The road goes upward towards the light; but the laden traveler may never reach the end of it.</b><br/><br/>The second book in Ursula Le Guin's venerated Earthsea series follows the story of young Tamar, who is taken from her parents at a young age and raised to be the High Priestess t</span>
  <span id="freeText10151891592848502744" style="display:none"><b>What she had begun to learn was the weight of liberty. Freedom is a heavy load, a great and strange burden for the spirit to undertake. It is not easy. It is not a gift given, but a choice made, and the choice may be a hard one. The road goes upward towards the light; but the laden traveler may never reach the end of it.</b><br /><br />The second book in Ursula Le Guin's venerated Earthsea series follows the story of young Tamar, who is taken from her parents at a young age and raised to be the High Priestess to the ancient Nameless Ones. Tamar's story is interesting but not especially compelling until about the midway point when it begins to tie in to the characters and events of its predecessor <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13642.A_Wizard_of_Earthsea__Earthsea_Cycle__1_" title="A Wizard of Earthsea (Earthsea Cycle #1) by Ursula K. Le Guin" rel="nofollow noopener">A Wizard of Earthsea</a>. As in the first book, Le Guin's beautiful prose and deftly drawn characters are underscored by the themes of duality and identity.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10151891592848502744" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText6492415367821735336" style="display:none"><i>And at the year's end she is taken to the Hall of the Throne and he name is given back to those who are her Masters, the Nameless Ones: for she is the nameless one, the Priestess Ever Reborn.</i><br /><br />Tenar is selected as a young child as the Priestess Reborn and taken from her family at the young age of 5 to become the guardian of the Tombs of Atuan. However, one day while walking the labyrinth of her domain, she comes across a young wizard, Sparrowhawk, searching for the treasure hidden there, the Ring of Erreth-Akbe. He brings with him magic, and makes her question everything she's been taught.<br /><br />This was a relatively quick read, and while it seems that it did take a while for the story to pick up the pace, Le Guin does a great job of setting up the story and the characters. <br /><br />It isn't exactly action packed, but the writing is beautiful and it makes me wonder what is to come in the books following in the series.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer2797858686682710222">Reread, for the first time in a long while. When I was thirteen I appreciated this book but I didn't like it as much as <i>Wizard</i> and <i>Farthest Shore</i>—there's less magic, less incident, and a lot more slow creeping dread. On this reread the dread itself became magical. And much as I love Ged, Tenar feels more... like she exists from the marrow out. I have a theory about this, but it's more of an essay-length theory than a Goodreads capsule review theory. In short, brilliant and deep.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Althea Ann" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Althea Ann" href="/user/show/3846743-althea-ann">Althea Ann</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer6933330998846628308">One of my favorite books of all time - I've probably read this one over two dozen times. <br/>It's a deceptively simple story, simple in the way that all truths are simple, allegorical in that it can be applied to all of our lives. it's a story of growing up, of claiming freedom and independence, and all the fear and pain and joy that can accompany that. But it's also just the story of Tenar, called Arha, priestess of the Nameless Ones and mistress of the Undertomb - a girl who believes herself hard,</span>
  <span id="freeText6933330998846628308" style="display:none">One of my favorite books of all time - I've probably read this one over two dozen times. <br />It's a deceptively simple story, simple in the way that all truths are simple, allegorical in that it can be applied to all of our lives. it's a story of growing up, of claiming freedom and independence, and all the fear and pain and joy that can accompany that. But it's also just the story of Tenar, called Arha, priestess of the Nameless Ones and mistress of the Undertomb - a girl who believes herself hard, cold and powerful. And it is the story of Ged, the young wizard who finds himself at her mercy. It is a story of finding compassion, and how strength lies not in the dark and restricted ways, or in bringing death - but instead lies in having the courage to admit vulnerability, in daring to step outside all that is taught and to find ones way to the light.<br />The writing is just beautiful - some of the descriptive passages here are unparallelled. A perfect book.</span>

<div id="review_3353562787" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3353562787" />
    <a title="Lea" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/38055348-lea"><img alt="Lea" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1502202788p2/38055348.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3353562787?book_show_action=true">May 24, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lea" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lea" href="/user/show/38055348-lea">Lea</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38055348-lea?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38055348-lea?shelf=ebook">ebook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38055348-lea?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3353562787" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16306447606830025488">Le Guin fascinates me again! This is another highly intelligent and well-thought-out fantasy story. I loved that in this sequel, Ged wasn't the only main character and that we are introduced to Tenar, a very well developed female character. The story starts with a young girl named Tenar, with given name Arha, and she is separated at a young age from her parents to become a High priestess in the temple of the Unnamed ones. So this is not just story about young wizard Ged, it is mostly about Tenar</span>
  <span id="freeText16306447606830025488" style="display:none">Le Guin fascinates me again! This is another highly intelligent and well-thought-out fantasy story. I loved that in this sequel, Ged wasn't the only main character and that we are introduced to Tenar, a very well developed female character. The story starts with a young girl named Tenar, with given name Arha, and she is separated at a young age from her parents to become a High priestess in the temple of the Unnamed ones. So this is not just story about young wizard Ged, it is mostly about Tenar, as Ged is a visitor in her world. The story could also be read as the maturation of the Anima aspect in Ged, as well as the development of the womanhood of Tenar. Le Guin her demonstrates the capability of describing both men and women's path of psychological development - for each integration of the opposite sex aspect is inevitable for maturity, and unity of both male and female parts of the psyche are inevitable for the process of individuation, to find a true, authentic Self. <br /><br /><i>''You have set us both free," he said. "Alone, no one wins freedom. ''</i><br /><br />Without each other, both Ged and Tenar would be destroyed in some way. I highly respect that Ged and Tenar played such a big part in each other's lives in liberation and progress without being romantically involved in any phase. I love that their unity is represented in two parts of one ring as a higher spiritual principle that brings peace to mankind, rather than physical unity in sexuality that brings relief only to them. Interestingly, the previous book could be interpreted as Ged's beginning of the process of individuation in the integration of the shadow aspect. According to Jung, the next step in individuation is the maturation of the Anima - the female aspect of the hero. When a male hero has to undergo the development of the female aspect he is always stripped down from his manpower - as Ged lost his staff and clothing in the Labyrinth of Thombs. It is also interesting that the Labyrinths are completely in dark - that can represent unconsciousness. Ged can bring light to one level, but it eventually goes out. Tenar is the one that knows the maps of the labyrinth by hearts and sees in the dark - Ged would be completely and utterly lost without her. Symbolically man principle is represented in Apollo - light, reason, logic and consciousness, but the female principle, for example Aphrodite, reigns in unconsciousness, the world of impulses, instincts, fertility and creativity. This is not the first story in with male hero is guided out of the labyrinth of unconsciousness by the help of female wisdom - we have the same situation with Ariadne that gave Theseus gift that guided him through the labyrinth of Minotaur, and enabled him to find a way to defeat the monster.<br /><br />Ged can also be viewed as Tenar's Animus. He helped Tenar by giving her the light and truth od reason, a perspective of the outer world she didn't know about, that helped her to break out of the dominion of a repressive matriarchal world she lived in - priestess were the servants of dark forces, but had the highest power in the community - men only excited in their word as slaves with cut out tongues or eunuchs that served them - both representing castration (in one way striping men of power to produce form and meaning by language, one of stripping men of sexual power and potency). That is the guiding principle of the toxic matriarchal world - men have no purpose or value, they only exist to be dominated and serve the feminine (mirror principle can be found in a destructive form of patriarchy - but that is by far more talked about when on an individual level both pathologies are common). By creating a real human relationship with a man, Ged, Tenar violated the law of matriarchy and had to be punished. Now in this new state of being she could stay and dye, or run and break free. As <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/74468.Erich_Neumann" title="Erich Neumann" rel="nofollow noopener">Neumann</a> said in the study of the psychological development of women, in her path to maturity women can use matriarchal principles to take control and be empowered and independent, but the key is not to integrate the destructive part that hates and wants to destroy the masculine, as Tenar in the end successfully did. As Tenar did with Ged, women can take constructive parts of masculinity without harming the masculine. Ged helped Tenar by calling her by her true name, she guided him through unconsciousness and he guided her through consciousness about her identity. <br /><br /><i>"You told me to show you something worth seeing. I show you yourself.''</i><br /><br />Tenar was only known as Arha (with meant the eaten one - her true identity was eaten by rules and expectations of others, mostly the ruling principle of a tyrannical mother) and only Ged had the knowledge of her true name, and hearing her true name gave her the freedom to be true and authentic self. She critically questioned the world she lived in and came to her unique perspective and conclusion, and in the process broke out of the darkness of unconsciousness and inauthenticity. Hearing her true name created the connection to true self - her own identity and desires outside of what is expected of her to due, and what other people told her she is made for. <br /><br /><i>"I am Tenar," she said, not aloud, and she shook with cold, and terror, and exultation, there under the open, sunwashed sky. "I have my name back. I am Tenar!''</i><br /><br />But even freedom and consciousness have their weight and are not easy to deal with, and in the end this is just a starting point for both Ged and Tenar.<br /><br /><i>''What she had begun to learn was the weight of liberty. Freedom is a heavy load, a great and strange burden for the spirit to undertake. It is not easy. It is not a gift given, but a choice made, and the choice may be a hard one. The road goes upward towards the light; but the laden traveler may never reach the end of it.''</i><br /><br />Le Guin's capability to write such an enlightened but simple story astonishes me. Can’t wait to read the rest of the series as this is such a brilliant synthesis of a book that is highly enjoyable, yet profound.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16306447606830025488" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_281472644" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/281472644" />
    <a title="Nicky" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/223837-nicky"><img alt="Nicky" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1308224598p2/223837.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/281472644?book_show_action=true">Feb 21, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nicky" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nicky" href="/user/show/223837-nicky">Nicky</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780553273311" href="/book/show/1173006.The_Tombs_of_Atuan">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/223837-nicky?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer281472644" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17918540952361753044">I think The Tombs of Atuan has always been my favourite of the Earthsea cycle. I said to someone recently that the quiet moment where Tenar watches Ged sleeping, and there's a thistle by his hand, and the world just seems so strange, was somehow a moment that perfectly defines Le Guin's work for me. That quietness, that moment of clarity, of seeing-things-anew...<br/><br/>If nothing else, that's the feeling I get when I read her work.<br/><br/>The Tombs of Atuan begins to redress the balance of the world Le Guin cr</span>
  <span id="freeText17918540952361753044" style="display:none">I think The Tombs of Atuan has always been my favourite of the Earthsea cycle. I said to someone recently that the quiet moment where Tenar watches Ged sleeping, and there's a thistle by his hand, and the world just seems so strange, was somehow a moment that perfectly defines Le Guin's work for me. That quietness, that moment of clarity, of seeing-things-anew...<br /><br />If nothing else, that's the feeling I get when I read her work.<br /><br />The Tombs of Atuan begins to redress the balance of the world Le Guin created. Where there were no women before, she's set the second work in a female-driven society, where <i>only</i> women have power. And she doesn't fall into any kind of trap about black-and-white good-and-bad, either: Thar is positive, even though she's grim, and Arha/Tenar has her moments of darkness as well as of light.<br /><br />And of course, as far as I'm concerned, prose-wise Le Guin can do no wrong.<br /><br />It might take me a while to get round to reading The Farthest Shore, as that's my least favourite of the sequence. I'm wondering if maturity will help.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17918540952361753044" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_141235347" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/141235347" />
    <a title="Jeanette (Again)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/796425-jeanette-again"><img alt="Jeanette (Again)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1304653755p2/796425.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/141235347?book_show_action=true">Jan 16, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeanette (Again)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeanette (Again)" href="/user/show/796425-jeanette-again">Jeanette (Again)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/796425-jeanette-again?shelf=all-fiction">all-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/796425-jeanette-again?shelf=four-star-fiction">four-star-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/796425-jeanette-again?shelf=young-adult-books">young-adult-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/796425-jeanette-again?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer141235347" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16416098222406579216">This is the second book in the Earthsea Cycle. Plot-wise it's not as good as <i>A Wizard of Earthsea</i>, but the writing is better. It has such wonderful fluidity that I read the entire book in just a few hours. For that I can give it four stars, though the story lacks the magic and adventure of the first book. <br/><br/>Tenar is taken from her family at the age of five and given to "the Dark Ones" (aka "the Nameless Ones") at the age of six. The belief is that they eat her soul, and thereafter she belongs to t</span>
  <span id="freeText16416098222406579216" style="display:none">This is the second book in the Earthsea Cycle. Plot-wise it's not as good as <i>A Wizard of Earthsea</i>, but the writing is better. It has such wonderful fluidity that I read the entire book in just a few hours. For that I can give it four stars, though the story lacks the magic and adventure of the first book. <br /><br />Tenar is taken from her family at the age of five and given to "the Dark Ones" (aka "the Nameless Ones") at the age of six. The belief is that they eat her soul, and thereafter she belongs to them and is nameless. She is no longer Tenar but Arha "the eaten one," First Priestess of the Nameless Ones. As with any religion, Arha is brainwashed into believing these gods must be feared and placated, without proof of their existence. When she encounters the concept of "unfaith," it is a surprise and a wonder to her.<br /><br />Arha spends her youth mostly in solitude and mostly underground, in the Undertomb and the Labyrinth. No light is allowed in the Undertomb, and no males except eunuchs may enter. One day Arha sees a light in the Undertomb and discovers, GASP!, a man walking around down there. As First Priestess, it is her duty to have him killed for violating that space. But, <i>mirabile dictu</i>, she begins to think for herself. She wonders why the gods she serves have not unleashed their wrath upon the man with the light. She has always been taught that disaster will follow if light is brought into the Undertomb.<br /><br />Arha traps the man underground and amuses herself by spying on him while she decides what to do. The man turns out to be Ged/Sparrowhawk the wizard. He has come to the Tombs of Atuan seeking part of a Lost Rune which will bring peace to Earthsea. He shares with Arha his hard-won wisdom and helps her see that the Nameless Ones are not gods, but dark forces to be avoided. Together they escape to the West, where Arha once again becomes Tenar. <br /><br />When they set sail and Tenar knows she is truly free, she feels sorrow and loss rather than joy. She grieves for the waste of her life given to false gods and useless rituals, and fears a future where she must make her own decisions. <br /> An exceptional passage from page 173 speaks to the burden of freedom after escaping enslavement to a belief system: <br /><br /><i>"She wept in pain, because she was free. <br />What she had begun to learn was the weight of liberty. Freedom is a heavy load, a great and strange burden for the spirit to undertake. It is not easy. It is not a gift given, but a choice made, and the choice may be a hard one. The road goes upward towards the light, but the laden traveler may never reach the end of it."</i> </span>
  <a data-text-id="16416098222406579216" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer18232467029822568329">In reality, this book should have bored me. The vast bulk of the story is told in a very limited worldview, underground in a cave. The slave girl whose point-of-view the reader follows is an unreliable narrator. <br/><br/>But somehow, Ursula Le Guin's writing voice just captivates me.   <span id="freeText18232467029822568329" style="display:none">In reality, this book should have bored me. The vast bulk of the story is told in a very limited worldview, underground in a cave. The slave girl whose point-of-view the reader follows is an unreliable narrator. <br /><br />But somehow, Ursula Le Guin's writing voice just captivates me. She's has a rich voice, similar to Tolkien and Lewis. She believes in her worlds so much that they spring into reality while you read. Her style is beautiful. <br /><br />I was surprised that she used an entirely different character's POV to continue Ged's story. I kept waiting for it to switch to him, but somehow it worked. On to Book Three! <br /><br />I'd rate this book a PG for scary situations and images and dark magic and spirits.</span>

<div id="review_79903489" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/79903489" />
    <a title="Kara Babcock" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1188122-kara-babcock"><img alt="Kara Babcock" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1580776593p2/1188122.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/79903489?book_show_action=true">Dec 04, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kara Babcock" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kara Babcock" href="/user/show/1188122-kara-babcock">Kara Babcock</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780553273311" href="/book/show/1173006.The_Tombs_of_Atuan">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=2011-best10">2011-best10</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=2011-read">2011-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=own">own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer79903489&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer79903489" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18430989252777931449">Damn you, Ursula K. Le Guin, for writing books that are so good, sometimes they hurt.<br/><br/>Like <em>A Wizard of Earthsea</em>, <em>The Tombs of Atuan</em> follows a single protagonist over a long span of her life. Tenar, identified as the reincarnation of the First Priestess of the Nameless Ones, is taken from her parents at a young age. Her soul ceremonially consumed by the Nameless Ones, Tenar becomes Arha, "the Eaten One," and paradoxically nameless herself. She grows up among other priestesses and eunuchs. And she'</span>
  <span id="freeText18430989252777931449" style="display:none">Damn you, Ursula K. Le Guin, for writing books that are so good, sometimes they hurt.<br><br>Like <em>A Wizard of Earthsea</em>, <em>The Tombs of Atuan</em> follows a single protagonist over a long span of her life. Tenar, identified as the reincarnation of the First Priestess of the Nameless Ones, is taken from her parents at a young age. Her soul ceremonially consumed by the Nameless Ones, Tenar becomes Arha, "the Eaten One," and paradoxically nameless herself. She grows up among other priestesses and eunuchs. And she's a very bored girl. She goes through the motions of learning the ways of the High Priestess, sacrificing prisoners to the Nameless Ones, etc., but her heart isn't in it. Then one day, a wizard from the Archipelago shows up in the Labyrinth beneath the tombs, a place where only Arha is allowed to go.<br><br>This wizard is, of course, Ged, the protagonist of the previous book. I'm sure that if the entire book were from Ged's perspective the story of how he sneaked into the Labyrinth to steal something would sound a lot better; as it is, he comes off as a bit of a mysterious jerk. Yet Ged's arrival is the event that changes everything. Locked in the dark tombs with little light and precious little food or water, he does something that might seem meaningless to most of us, but to Arha, it is the most potent act possible: he gives her back her name. Taken from her by the priestesses, Ged divines it and utters it almost casually at a parting, and in so doing he returns to Arha her true identity as Tenar, setting her off on the path to liberation.<br><br>Now that I have re-read the first two books, it seems so obvious to me that the entire <em>Earthsea</em> series is about, among other things, identity. Generally, it is a world where identity is part of the fabric of magic: to know something's true name is to know the thing, to have command over it. In <em>A Wizard of Earthsea</em>, Ged finds out who he is even as he learns more about the gebbeth hunting him, a creature that almost is <em>not</em>. And thanks to his adventures and deeds during and after that book, he gets all these additional titles bestowed upon him—dragonlord, and the like—for which he never asked. It's the same in <em>The Tombs of Atuan</em>; if anything the motif is much more pronounced. Tenar's identity is stolen from her in childhood, and her relief to have it back came like a sucker punch to my gut:<br><br><blockquote>It was not long past sunrise, a fair winter's day. The sky was yellowish, very clear. High up, so high he caught the sunlight and burned like a fleck of gold, a bird was circling, a hawk or desert eagle.<br><br>"I am Tenar," she said, not aloud, and she shook with cold, and terror, and exultation, there under the sunwashed sky. "I have my name back. I am Tenar!"<br><br>The golden fleck veered westwards towards the mountains, out of sight. Sunrise gilded the eaves of the Small House. Sheep bells clanked, down in the folds. The smells of woodsmoke and buckwheat porridge from the kitchen chimneys drifted on the fine, fresh wind.<br><br>"I am so hungry.… How did he know? How did he know my name? … Oh, I've got to eat, I'm so hungry.…"<br><br>She pulled up her hood and ran off to breakfast.</blockquote><br><br>It is as if having her true name restored to her has re-awakened her entire being, given her a new life. Everything is fresh, more real—hence the hunger, the need for energy to confront this wonderful new world. Just like that, Le Guin smites us with a sense of joy that has heretofore been totally absent from Tenar's life.<br><br>A lesser writer might have ended the book after Tenar and Ged escape the Tombs of Atuan. Maybe there would be a coda explaining how they lived happily ever after, but that would be it. Le Guin, however, does not succumb to this temptation for the fairy tale ending. After they escape, their trials are <em>not</em> over. Tenar does not fall into Ged's arms, swooning over the hero who has rescued her from her spiritual imprisonment. Their journey across Atuan to the sea is slow, and at times it is as precarious as their time deep in the tombs. Tenar's trust in Ged is nascent and uneasy, made all the more difficult by psychic warfare on the part of the Nameless Ones. She comes close to <em>killing</em> Ged, but he responds to her only with kindness and reassurance:<br><br><blockquote>"Now," he said, "now we're away, now we're clear, we're clean gone, Tenar. Do you feel it?"<br><br>She did feel it. A dark hand had let go its lifelong hold upon her heart. But she did not feel joy, as she had in the mountains. She put her head down in her arms and cried, and her cheeks were salt and wet. She cried for the waste of her years in bondage to a useless evil. She wept in pain, because she was free.<br><br>What she had begun to learn was the weight of liberty. Freedom is a heavy load, a great and strange burden for the spirit to undertake. It is not easy. It is not a gift given, but a choice made, and the choice may be a hard one. The road goes upward towards the light; but the laden traveler may never reach the end of it.</blockquote><br><br>This is why Le Guin is so awesome: even though she's telling us a story, a work of fiction, she never lies to us. She shows us the joy, but she also shows us the sorrow that accompanies it like shadow accompanies light. And she does not cheapen the significance of Tenar's journey—whether it's the freedom she has gained or the life she has lost—by trying to simplify, to pander, or to sex it up.<br><br>Speaking of which, re-reading <em>The Tombs of Atuan</em>, even more than my re-reading of <em>A Wizard of Earthsea</em>, has only increased my ire toward the Sci-Fi Channel miniseries. The whitewashing of the cast is regrettable, but now I have a much better perspective on how they butchered the story. The miniseries uses material from both of these books, but rather than connecting them chronologically, which could make sense, the miniseries conflates them. Ged's battle against the gebbeth is combined with his search for the Ring of Erreth-Akbe. But Tenar's story is almost unrecognizable: everyone <em>calls</em> her Tenar, none of this "Eaten One" business, and she's treated more like an uppity novice than any kind of reincarnation of the High Priestess. And as the series draws to a close, Ged and Tenar meet up and reunite the two halves of the Ring and bring peace, etc., and there's no crying. There is no <em>significance</em> to Tenar's journey—pretty much there is almost no character development, aside from Tenar's change of allegiance. There is no depth, and if Gavin Scott had ever encountered the word "nuance" before, he certainly did not bother to look up its definition.<br><br>I did not remember this book as well as I remembered <em>A Wizard of Earthsea</em>, though I'm sure I've read it before. So I began re-reading it with the expectation that it would be good but not in the way its predecessor is. Instead, I find myself adding a <em>third</em> Le Guin book to my shelf of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/list/1188122-ben-babcock?shelf=favourites" rel="nofollow noopener">all-time favourites</a>, an honour I do not bestow lightly. But <em>The Tombs of Atuan</em> <strong>is just that good</strong>. It's more than good: it's beautiful and poignant and strong. Shame on you, Le Guin, shame!<br><br><strong>My Reviews of the <em>Earthsea</em> series:</strong><br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/158631337" rel="nofollow noopener">← <em>A Wizard of Earthsea</em></a> | <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/162867869" rel="nofollow noopener"><em>The Farthest Shore</em> →</a><br><br><a href="http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img alt="Creative Commons License" width="88" height="31" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380238653i/681550.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="18430989252777931449" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_109730003" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/109730003" />
    <a title="Ian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2465351-ian"><img alt="Ian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1324174664p2/2465351.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/109730003?book_show_action=true">Jul 02, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ian" href="/user/show/2465351-ian">Ian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2465351-ian?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2465351-ian?shelf=love-strong-women">love-strong-women</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2465351-ian?shelf=all-time-favorites">all-time-favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer109730003" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5139797475100119262">What wonderful and vivid imagery this book contains. Imagery with power for those readers who can identify with the central figure, Arha/Tenar, especially those who are faced with challenges in their own lives similar to those she confronts. I found imagery of the mystery and challenge of self-discovery; imagery of the conflict and dual-nature within humanity and within individuals; imagery of loss, dedication, loneliness, and self-denial; imagery of childhood, adolescence, and adulthood; imager</span>
  <span id="freeText5139797475100119262" style="display:none">What wonderful and vivid imagery this book contains. Imagery with power for those readers who can identify with the central figure, Arha/Tenar, especially those who are faced with challenges in their own lives similar to those she confronts. I found imagery of the mystery and challenge of self-discovery; imagery of the conflict and dual-nature within humanity and within individuals; imagery of loss, dedication, loneliness, and self-denial; imagery of childhood, adolescence, and adulthood; imagery of gender roles and expectations and even … (dare I say it?) … imagery of sex and the loss of one's … innocence.<br /><br />In a sense, <i>The Tombs of Atuan</i> is the female counterpart to <i>A Wizard of Earthsea</i>. Don't get me wrong: I don't think <i>The Tombs of Atuan</i> is <u>merely</u> the female counterpart to its predecessor; it just seems like the obvious point of comparison. Both books have much to say about finding one's self and one's way in the world, the first through a male character, Ged, and the second through a female character, Tenar. I can't say that I identified with Tenar nearly as well as I identified with Ged, but that's okay. I didn't enjoy the second book any less, I simply enjoyed it in different ways.<br /><br /><i>The Tombs of Atuan</i> is a demonstration of UKL's gifts as a writer: she can make abstract concepts feel tangible, and familiar or overwrought concepts feel new and refreshing. The <i>Earthsea</i> books are so, so much better than I expected. My only regret is that it took me so long to get to them. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5139797475100119262" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2860427624" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2860427624" />
    <a title="Tanzila Tabassum Zisha (Annabel Lee) " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36193941-tanzila-tabassum-zisha-annabel-lee"><img alt="Tanzila Tabassum Zisha (Annabel Lee) " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1444369916p2/36193941.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2860427624?book_show_action=true">Jul 03, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tanzila Tabassum Zisha (Annabel Lee) " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tanzila Tabassum Zisha (Annabel Lee) " href="/user/show/36193941-tanzila-tabassum-zisha-annabel-lee">Tanzila Tabassum Zisha (Annabel Lee) </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/25328608-the-tombs-of-atuan">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36193941-tanzila-tabassum-zisha-annabel-lee?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2860427624" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4940933045084280985">Ursula K. Le Guin might be the only author whose afterwords or author's notes I read with almost as much affection as I read her stories with. <br/>This second installment in the Earthsea Cycle is majorly about a girl named Tenar trapped in a temple as a priestess of dark powers. Much like the first one, it's written in a dense language with even less dialogue so it feels like a longer book. But unlike the first one, this has little world-building. It is more focused on the girl protagonist's interna</span>
  <span id="freeText4940933045084280985" style="display:none">Ursula K. Le Guin might be the only author whose afterwords or author's notes I read with almost as much affection as I read her stories with. <br />This second installment in the Earthsea Cycle is majorly about a girl named Tenar trapped in a temple as a priestess of dark powers. Much like the first one, it's written in a dense language with even less dialogue so it feels like a longer book. But unlike the first one, this has little world-building. It is more focused on the girl protagonist's internal conflicts as a devoted server of the dark against her own sane, human self. The book is largely confined inside the tomb temple where Tenar is trained to be a cold-hearted servant of the Nameless Ones up until the last portion when Ged from the previous book arrives bringing the final choices for Tenar.<br />I loved reading about Tenar/Arha's growing up as the One Priestess. She tried to fulfil her "Priestess of the Dark Powers" role and act as expected from her from the beginning and even though it was obvious she would break free from this place later- it still seemed there was a streak of cruelty in her heart more often than not.<br />My criticism so far is her books are very short and especially The Tombs of Atuan is a little too conflict-free (not counting the internal ones). There was a sense of great danger after Ged's arrival to Arha's escape but it all got solved too easily. <br />Still, this might be a good starting point for anyone who wants to start delving into epic fantasy without slogging through thousands of pages.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4940933045084280985" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <a title="Clouds" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9374686-clouds"><img alt="Clouds" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1514641128p2/9374686.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/349514697?book_show_action=true">Jun 16, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Clouds" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Clouds" href="/user/show/9374686-clouds">Clouds</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer349514697" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4020164585151537285"><a href='http://www.theaceofclouds.com/' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380317319i/689370.gif' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/>
</a><br/><b>Following the resounding success of my <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/story/show/332414-reading-lists?chapter=2' rel='nofollow noopener'>Locus Quest</a>, I faced a dilemma: which reading list to follow it up with? Variety is the spice of life, so I’ve decided to diversify and pursue six different lists simultaneously. This book falls into my <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/story/show/332414-reading-lists?chapter=3' rel='nofollow noopener'>LOCUS FANTASY</a> list.<br/><br/>As the Locus Sci-Fi Award winners list treated me so kindly, I figure I’ll trust those same good folk to pick me some stars in their sister-list, the Locus Fantasy Award winners.</b><br/><br/>Le Guin and I continue to rub along well-enough, without </span>
  <span id="freeText4020164585151537285" style="display:none"><a href="http://www.theaceofclouds.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380317319i/689370.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><b>Following the resounding success of my <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/story/show/332414-reading-lists?chapter=2" rel="nofollow noopener">Locus Quest</a>, I faced a dilemma: which reading list to follow it up with? Variety is the spice of life, so I’ve decided to diversify and pursue six different lists simultaneously. This book falls into my <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/story/show/332414-reading-lists?chapter=3" rel="nofollow noopener">LOCUS FANTASY</a> list.<br><br>As the Locus Sci-Fi Award winners list treated me so kindly, I figure I’ll trust those same good folk to pick me some stars in their sister-list, the Locus Fantasy Award winners.</b><br><br>Le Guin and I continue to rub along well-enough, without becoming friends. I've come to respect what she does, while acknowledging it just doesn't push my buttons.<br><br>When I scan the many 5-star reviews from Goodreads friends whose opinions I respect (and normally agree with) I find myself in the apathetic minority with Earthsea. They're on the cusp between "it was OK" and "like it", and I round that 2.5 up to 3 because I have a generous soul.<br><br>This is the story of the Eaten One, the reincarnated Priestess who eventually rediscovers free-will and absconds with Sparrowhawk. I was struggling with it until Sparrowhawk rocks-up - the Priestess on her own is way too passive/drifty until that point (a common issue of mine with Le Guin's work). Even after the Wizard is on the scene, there's a crux period of dilemma/decision for the Priestess which had me glued to the page, and then we're back to another drifty sequence on the journey home...<br><br>Le Guin excels at quiet moments. I liked <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/281472644" rel="nofollow noopener">Nikki's summary</a>:<blockquote>
  <i>I said to someone recently that the quiet moment where [the Pristess] watches [the Wizard] sleeping, and there's a thistle by his hand, and the world just seems so strange, was somehow a moment that perfectly defines Le Guin's work for me. <b>That quietness, that moment of clarity, of seeing-things-anew.</b></i>
</blockquote>A lot of the story is about getting into the Priestess' headspace, so we can appreciate that moment of clarity fully... but the payoff just didn't balance out the legwork for me. Like climbing a hill to see the view... and seeing some more hills and trees and thinking "yeah, pretty an' all, but I've seen prettier paintings that didn't leave my legs aching..."<br><br>Kudos to all those who love the Earthsea series - I will read the next couple of books as I already have them in an omnibus - but unless the next two feature a big swing in style, don't expect to list me amongst the converts!<br><br><b>After this I read: </b><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/785239683" rel="nofollow noopener">Skin Game</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="4020164585151537285" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1527036770" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1527036770" />
    <a title="Sumant" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21982745-sumant"><img alt="Sumant" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1373196404p2/21982745.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1527036770?book_show_action=true">Jan 26, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sumant" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sumant" href="/user/show/21982745-sumant">Sumant</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21982745-sumant?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21982745-sumant?shelf=earth-sea-cycle">earth-sea-cycle</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1527036770" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10868002172213956312">The second book in <b>Earth sea cycle</b> centers on coming of age tale of a young woman <b>Tenar</b>, who is dedicated in the service of nameless ones. The book for most part was really slow for me, and started to pick up when our wizard <b>Ged</b> from the first book makes an appearance and manages to change the world of <b>Tenar</b>.<br/><br/>Some of the strong points of the book are<br/><br/>1. <b>Simple story.</b><br/>2. <b>Narration by Ursula K Le Guin.</b><br/><br/>Some of the weak points of book are<br/><br/>1. <b>The rivalry between Kossil & Tenar seems hollow.</b><br/>2. <b>The book is</b></span>
  <span id="freeText10868002172213956312" style="display:none">The second book in <b>Earth sea cycle</b> centers on coming of age tale of a young woman <b>Tenar</b>, who is dedicated in the service of nameless ones. The book for most part was really slow for me, and started to pick up when our wizard <b>Ged</b> from the first book makes an appearance and manages to change the world of <b>Tenar</b>.<br /><br />Some of the strong points of the book are<br /><br />1. <b>Simple story.</b><br />2. <b>Narration by Ursula K Le Guin.</b><br /><br />Some of the weak points of book are<br /><br />1. <b>The rivalry between Kossil &amp; Tenar seems hollow.</b><br />2. <b>The book is too slow.</b><br /><br />Let me elaborate on the above points<br /><br />1. <b>Simple story.</b><br /><br />The book tells the story of <b>Tenar</b> who has been born on the same moon day when the high priestess of <b>Nameless ones</b> at the tombs dies, due to this she gets chosen to be the next high priestess in the <b>Tombs of Atuan</b>.<br /><br />Taken away from her family at the age of five, she quickly starts believing that the <b>tombs</b> are her only home, she is guided in her priestly duties by <b>Thar &amp; Kossil</b>. She also befriends an eunuch known as <b>Manan</b> who is charged with protecting her.<br /><br />As she starts coming of age she starts realizing that all is not well between the duties she has been charged with, as it consists of doing acts of cruelty towards others, but instead of voicing her concerns she starts going into her shell and starts finding solace inside the dark world of tombs. Her perception changes when she encounters <b>Ged</b> in the tombs, initially she cannot believe the fact that a man has come inside the the tombs, as it goes against all she has been taught by the priestesses, but when she starts interacting with him she comes to know that the tombs are not really what they are told to be.<br /><br />2. <b>Narration by Ursula K Le Guin.</b><br /><br />I listened to this book on audio and the voice of <b>Tenar</b> was done by <b>Le Guin</b> and she manages to convey all the emotions of <b>Tenar</b> perfectly, it would not be far fetched to say that due to her narration I kept on with the book as it was really slow for my taste.<br /><br />Some of the weak points of book are<br /><br />1. <b>The rivalry between Kossil &amp; Tenar seems hollow.</b><br /><br />Now the ownership of the <b>tombs</b> is shown to be claimed by priestess of two different sects, one who worship the <b>God king</b> and other which serve the <b>Nameless ones</b>. Now <b>Kossil</b> is shown as serving the <b>God king</b> and always shown as misguiding <b>Tenar</b>. But I think except some incidents, their rivalry seemed a bit half cooked to me, and considering the length of the book <b>Le Guin</b> could have focused on this rivalry more.<br /><br />2. <b>The book is too slow.</b><br /><br />As I have already said the book drags a lot after <b>Tenar</b> is established as the priestess in <b>tombs</b> and only picks up when <b>Ged</b> arrives.<br /><br />I give this book 3/5 stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10868002172213956312" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div class='carouselRow' style='width: 3600px'>
<ul>
<li class='cover' id='bookCover_55319208'>
<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/55319208-m-ngata"><img alt="Mångata" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1600022382l/55319208._SY160_.jpg" />
</a>

  
<script type="text/javascript" charset="utf-8">
//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('hLWXTI1/dc6xKXAW81YJqBH91F2+U1YfkB8YS5oef7t47V+DhR1cAvPamniZyK79A93IIk+112nQDyVEFI2qhw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('DsZ960rgDsN4CyKHmcNtMP0Q0RfXTkPY3Wsm0Ac2fyXynrUkQoInDzr4yOnzXcpl7zDNaCaowq6dexvfiaWqGQ==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

Whovian fins al moll de l’os, lovecraftià d’esperit, montypythonesc com a filosofia de vida. Without sunlight, clean water, or arable land, and with limited stockpiles of supplies, there will be war all across the Stillness: a battle royale of nations not for power or territory, but simply for the basic resources necessary to get through the long dark night.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/874602.Ursula_K_Le_Guin">About Ursula K. Le Guin</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/874602.Ursula_K_Le_Guin"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1244291425p3/874602.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/874602.Ursula_K_Le_Guin">Ursula K. Le Guin
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
18,051
followers
</div>

</div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer6428918866265788913">Ursula K. Le Guin published twenty-two novels, eleven volumes of short stories, four collections of essays, twelve books for children, six volumes of poetry and four of translation, and has received many awards: Hugo, Nebula, National Book Award, PEN-Malamud, etc. Her recent publications include the novel <i>Lavinia</i>, an essay collection, <i>Cheek by Jowl</i>, and <i>The Wild Girls</i>. She lived in Portland, Orego</span>
  <span id="freeText6428918866265788913" style="display:none">Ursula K. Le Guin published twenty-two novels, eleven volumes of short stories, four collections of essays, twelve books for children, six volumes of poetry and four of translation, and has received many awards: Hugo, Nebula, National Book Award, PEN-Malamud, etc. Her recent publications include the novel <i>Lavinia</i>, an essay collection, <i>Cheek by Jowl</i>, and <i>The Wild Girls</i>. She lived in Portland, Oregon.<br /><br />She was known for her treatment of gender (<i>The Left Hand of Darkness</i>, <i>The Matter of Seggri</i>), political systems (<i>The Telling</i>, <i>The Dispossessed</i>) and difference/otherness in any other form. Her interest in non-Western philosophies was reflected in works such as "Solitude" and <i>The Telling</i> but even more interesting are her imagined societies, often mixing traits extracted from her profound knowledge of anthropology acquired from growing up with her father, the famous anthropologist, Alfred Kroeber. The Hainish Cycle reflects the anthropologist's experience of immersing themselves in new strange cultures since most of their main characters and narrators (Le Guin favoured the first-person narration) are envoys from a humanitarian organization, the Ekumen, sent to investigate or ally themselves with the people of a different world and learn their ways.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6428918866265788913" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
